        <p class="date">
         Mon Aug 11, 2014 at 03:16 AM PDT
        </p>
        <h2>
         <a href="/story/2014/08/11/1320245/-The-Pentagon-is-preparing-for-societal-collapse-due-to-Climate-Change" id="titleHref">
          The Pentagon is preparing for societal collapse due to Climate Change
         </a>
         <span style="white-space: nowrap">
         </span>
        </h2>
        <p class="author">
         <span>
          by
         </span>
         <a href="/user/Pakalolo">
          Pakalolo
         </a>
         <a alt="Follow this author" class="ajax type-user do-hotlist do-updateContent size-tiny off altTooltip" href="#?friend_id=175799&amp;is_stream=1">
          Follow
         </a>
        </p>
        <ul class="front-page-share clearfix">
         <li style="margin-left: 0px">
          <div class="fb-share-button" data-href="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2014/08/11/1320245/-The-Pentagon-is-preparing-for-societal-collapse-due-to-Climate-Change" data-layout="button_count">
          </div>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="twitter-share-button" data-count="horizontal" data-related="markos" data-text="The Pentagon is preparing for societal collapse due to Climate Change" data-url="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2014/08/11/1320245/-The-Pentagon-is-preparing-for-societal-collapse-due-to-Climate-Change" data-via="dailykos" href="https://twitter.com/share">
           Tweet
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="comments comment_count_with_new" data-comment_count="271" data-story_id="1320245" href="/story/2014/08/11/1320245/-The-Pentagon-is-preparing-for-societal-collapse-due-to-Climate-Change#comments">
           271 Comments
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </div>
       <div class="article-body">
        <div id="intro">
         <p>
          Most of us on this site are well aware that climate change impacts will exacerbate marginal living in poor and desperate nations particularly those in Africa, South Asia and the Middle East. These humanitarian and natural disaster impacts will be occurring in multiple places all around the world, possibly occurring all at once and on a scale human civilization has never seen before. But what about the developed world, particularly the United States?
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.cna.org/sites/default/files/news/FlipBooks/Climate%20Change%20web/flipviewerxpress.html">
           National Security and the threat of Climate Change
          </a>
          prepared by
          <a href="http://lauraepasqualeconsulting.com/2014/07/23/cna-report-on-national-security-and-the-accelerating-risks-of-climate-change/">
           CNA
          </a>
          and released in 2007 with an advisory board of 11 of the country's top retired generals and admirals summarizes those threats and how the military will need to adapt.
         </p>
         <p>
          The report finds that in the US, security threats will consist of an unimaginable need for worldwide humanitarian aid and increasing migrations to the US from our neighbors to the south. This does not take into account that we will be dealing with our own catastrophic climate change impacts. There will be scarcity of water and melting of glaciers which currently provide water for millions of people. Drought and decreased rainfall will cause more severe droughts and devastate food production. For example, the
          <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ogallala_Aquifer">
           Ogallala Aquifer
          </a>
          , which provides 27% of the irrigated land in this country, is very stressed from human impact. The water table level is down 100 feet in some areas. Three of the top grain producing states, Texas, Kansas and Nebraska each get 70 to 90% of their water from the Ogallala which will be stressed even further with the predicted lack of rainfall.
          <a href="http://www.scienceworldreport.com/articles/7771/20130626/colorado-river-will-flow-climate-change-possibility-megadrought.htm">
           The Colorado River Basin
          </a>
          , the source of water for 10's of millions of people, is already experiencing reduced river flow. The
          <a href="http://www.wmo.int/pages/index_en.html">
           World Meteorological Organization
          </a>
          predicts an increase in tropical hurricane speed strength and increased rainfall amounts potentially devastating coasts up and down the eastern seaboard, GoM, and the Caribbean as the climate continues to bake.
         </p>
         <p>
          From
          <a href="http://www.earthisland.org/journal/index.php/eij/article/we_are_being_watched/">
           Earth Island Journal
          </a>
          :
          <br/>
         </p>
         <blockquote>
          "As greenhouse gas emissions continue unchecked, opposition to the fossil fuel industry has taken on a more urgent and confrontational tone. Some anti-fracking activists have engaged in nonviolent civil disobedience and the protests against the Keystone XL tar sands pipeline have involved arrests at the White House. Environmentalists and civil libertarians worry that accusations of terrorism, even if completely unfounded, could undermine peaceful political protest. The mere possibility of surveillance could handicap environmental groups ability to achieve their political goals. You are painting the political opposition as supporters of terrorism to discredit them and cripple their ability to remain politically viable, says Mike German, an FBI special agent for 16 years who now works with the ACLU"
         </blockquote>
         Given this information the Department of Defense has created the
         <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minerva_Initiative">
          Minerva Initiative.
         </a>
         <p>
          "The Minerva Initiative is a DoD-sponsored, university-based social science research initiative launched by the Secretary of Defense that focuses on areas of strategic importance to U.S. national security policy. Its objectives are to foster and improve the Defense Departments social science intellectual capital in order to address future
          <strong>
           security challenges and to build bridges between the Department of Defense and the academic social science community
          </strong>
          . Minerva will do this by bringing together universities, research institutions, and individual scholars; and by supporting multidisciplinary and cross-institutional projects addressing specific topic areas determined by the Department."
         </p>
        </div>
        <p class="divider-doodle">
        </p>
        <div class="article-body" id="body">
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.theguardian.com/profile/nafeez-ahmed">
           Nafeez Ahmed
          </a>
          reports that "
          <a href="http://www.theguardian.com/environment/earth-insight/2013/jun/14/climate-change-energy-shocks-nsa-prism">
           NSA Prism is motivated in part by fears that environmentally-linked disasters could spur anti-government activism
          </a>
          ".
          <br/>
         </p>
         <blockquote>
          But why have Western security agencies developed such an unprecedented capacity to spy on their own
          <a href="http://www.wired.com/2013/06/nsa-prism-verizon-surveillance/">
           domestic populations
          </a>
          ? Since the 2008 economic crash, security agencies have increasingly spied on political activists, especially environmental groups, on behalf of corporate interests. This activity is linked to the last decade of US defence planning, which has been increasingly concerned by the risk of civil unrest at home triggered by catastrophic events linked to climate change, energy shocks or economic crisis - or all three.
          <p>
           "Unilateral changes to
           <a href="http://www.longislandpress.com/2013/05/14/u-s-military-power-grab-goes-into-effect/">
            US military laws
           </a>
           formally granted the Pentagon extraordinary powers to intervene in a domestic "emergency" or "civil disturbance"
          </p>
          <p>
           "Federal military commanders have the authority, in extraordinary emergency circumstances where prior authorization by the President is impossible and duly constituted local authorities are unable to control the situation, to engage temporarily in activities that are necessary to quell large-scale, unexpected civil disturbances."
          </p>
          <p>
           Other documents show that the "extraordinary emergencies" the Pentagon is worried about include a range of environmental and related disasters.
          </p>
          <p>
           In 2006, the
           <a href="http://georgewbush-whitehouse.archives.gov/nsc/nss/2006/sectionX.html">
            US National Security Strategy
           </a>
           warned that:
          </p>
          <p>
           "Environmental destruction, whether caused by human behavior or cataclysmic mega-disasters such as floods, hurricanes, earthquakes, or tsunamis. Problems of this scope may overwhelm the capacity of local authorities to respond, and may even overtax national militaries, requiring a larger international response."
          </p>
          <p>
           Two years later, the Department of Defense's (DoD)
           <a href="http://www.scribd.com/doc/6163953/US-Army-Strategy-2008-Perpetual-Warfare">
            Army Modernisation Strategy
           </a>
           described the arrival of a new "era of persistent conflict" due to competition for "depleting natural resources and overseas markets" fuelling "future resource wars over water, food and energy." The report predicted a resurgence of:
          </p>
          <p>
           "... anti-government and radical ideologies that potentially threaten government stability."
          </p>
          <p>
           In the same year, a report by the US Army's Strategic Studies Institute warned that a series of domestic crises could provoke large-scale civil unrest. The path to "disruptive domestic shock" could include traditional threats such as deployment of WMDs, alongside "catastrophic natural and human disasters" or "pervasive public health emergencies" coinciding with "unforeseen economic collapse." Such crises could lead to "loss of functioning political and legal order" leading to "purposeful domestic resistance or insurgency...
          </p>
          <p>
           "DoD might be forced by circumstances to put its broad resources at the disposal of civil authorities to contain and reverse violent threats to domestic tranquility. Under the most extreme circumstances, this might include use of military force against hostile groups inside the United States. Further, DoD would be, by necessity, an essential enabling hub for the continuity of political authority in a multi-state or nationwide civil conflict or disturbance."
          </p>
          <p>
           That year, the Pentagon had begun developing a 20,000 strong troop force who would be on-hand to respond to "domestic catastrophes" and civil unrest - the programme was reportedly based on a 2005 homeland security strategy which emphasised "preparing for multiple, simultaneous mass casualty incidents."
          </p>
          <p>
           The following year, a US Army-funded RAND Corp study called for a US force presence specifically to deal with civil unrest.
          </p>
          <p>
           Such fears were further solidified in a detailed 2010 study by the US Joint Forces Command - designed to inform "joint concept development and experimentation throughout the Department of Defense" - setting out the US military's definitive vision for future trends and potential global threats. Climate change, the study said, would lead to increased risk of:
          </p>
          <p>
           "... tsunamis, typhoons, hurricanes, tornadoes, earthquakes and other natural catastrophes... Furthermore, if such a catastrophe occurs within the United States itself - particularly when the nation's economy is in a fragile state or where US military bases or key civilian infrastructure are broadly affected - the damage to US security could be considerable."
          </p>
          <p>
           The study also warned of a
           <a href="http://www.theguardian.com/business/2010/apr/11/peak-oil-production-supply?guni=Article:in%20body%20link">
            possible shortfall
           </a>
           in global oil output by 2015:
          </p>
          <p>
           "A severe energy crunch is inevitable without a massive expansion of production and refining capacity. While it is difficult to predict precisely what economic, political, and strategic effects such a shortfall might produce, it surely would reduce the prospects for growth in both the developing and developed worlds. Such an economic slowdown would exacerbate other unresolved tensions."
          </p>
          <p>
           That year the DoD's Quadrennial Defense Review seconded such concerns, while recognising that "climate change, energy security, and economic stability are inextricably linked."
          </p>
         </blockquote>
         Privatization of intelligence just happens to coincide with "increasing domestic surveillance operations against political activists, particularly those linked to environmental and social justice protest groups".
         <br/>
         <blockquote>
          NSA whistleblower Edward Snowden was a computer systems administrator for
          <a href="http://www.theguardian.com/world/2013/jun/09/booz-allen-hamilton-edward-snowden?guni=Article:in%20body%20link">
           Booz Allen Hamilton
          </a>
          , where he directly handled the NSA's IT systems, including the Prism surveillance system. According to
          <a href="Booz%20Allen's%202011%20Annual%20Report">
           Booz Allen's 2011 Annual Report
          </a>
          , the corporation has overseen Unified Quest "for more than a decade" to help "military and civilian leaders envision the future."
         </blockquote>
         snip!
         <br/>
         <blockquote>
          <a href="http://www.salon.com/2013/04/03/dhs_had_policy_of_daily_spying_on_activists/">
           Department of Homeland Security documents
          </a>
          released in April prove a "systematic effort" by the agency "to surveil and disrupt peaceful demonstrations" linked to Occupy Wall Street, according to the Partnership for Civil Justice Fund (PCJF).
          <p>
           Similarly,
           <a href="http://www.salon.com/2013/01/02/the_irony_of_joint_fbi_private_sector_ows_policing/">
            FBI documents
           </a>
           confirmed "a strategic partnership between the FBI, the Department of Homeland Security and the private sector" designed to produce intelligence on behalf of "the corporate security community." A PCJF spokesperson remarked that the documents show "federal agencies functioning as a de facto intelligence arm of Wall Street and Corporate America."
          </p>
          <p>
           In particular, domestic surveillance has systematically targeted
           <a href="http://www.earthisland.org/journal/index.php/eij/article/we_are_being_watched/">
            peaceful environment activists
           </a>
           including anti-fracking activists across the US, such as the Gas Drilling Awareness Coalition, Rising Tide North America, the People's Oil &amp; Gas Collaborative, and Greenpeace.
          </p>
         </blockquote>
         I have often suspected that the increase in intelligence such as spying on American citizens, the crackdown on OWS and the militarization of our local police forces is no coincidence. The government is indeed preparing, with the help of the 1%, to crush the inevitable angry pushback against the government and the corporations once the climate change shit hits the fan.
         <p>
          Nafeez Ahmed concludes his article as follows.
          <br/>
         </p>
         <blockquote>
          The Pentagon knows that environmental, economic and other crises could provoke widespread public anger toward government and corporations in coming years. The revelations on the NSA's global surveillance programmes are just the latest indication that as business as usual creates instability at home and abroad, and as disillusionment with the status quo escalates, Western publics are being increasingly viewed as potential enemies that must be policed by the state.
         </blockquote>
         <iframe defang_allowfullscreen="" frameborder="0" height="315" src="//www.youtube.com/embed/0zb1u_ySWQc" width="560">
         </iframe>
         <p>
          The entire video is fascinating, but you can skip to the 4:37 mark for the discussion on the Pentagon's preparation for societal collapse due to climate change.
         </p>
        </div>
       </div>

         <div class="tooltipContents qtip-style-dk4_large qtip-pos-br">
          <p>
           <b>
            More Tagging tips:
           </b>
          </p>
          <p>
           A tag is a way to search for this diary. If someone is searching for "Barack Obama," is this a diary they'd be trying to find?
          </p>
          <p>
           Use a person's full name, without any title. Senator Obama may become President Obama, and Michelle Obama might run for office.
          </p>
          <p>
           If your diary covers an election or elected official, use election tags, which are generally the state abbreviation followed by the office. 
		CA-01 is the first district House seat. CA-Sen covers both senate races. NY-GOV covers the New York governor's race.
          </p>
          <p>
           Tags do not compound: that is, "education reform" is a completely different tag from "education". A tag like "reform" alone is probably not meaningful.
          </p>
         </div>
         <!-- &lt;h4 id="p_tagErrorMessage"&gt;You must enter at least one Tag for this Diary Entry:&lt;/h4&gt; -->

          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            the dod is preparing for social unrest in the USA
            <br/>
            caused by CC or what ever?
            <br/>
            I mean i know US intrests are high on their agenda
            <br/>
            and strategic planig is for sure the job of the dod.
            <br/>
            but the problems that could be caused by CC in the USA
            <br/>
            are not the issue of the military or are they.
            <br/>
            but i know it is a old talking point against deniers
            <br/>
            that even the DOD accepts CC
           </p>
           <blockquote>
            The Pentagon knows that environmental, economic and other crises could provoke widespread public anger toward government and corporations in coming years.
           </blockquote>
           so i ask again are these problems,
           <br/>
           problems the military have to deal with
           <br/>
           in the USA?
           <br/>
           really
           <br/>
           holly cluster f
           <br/>
           i guess i missed this link
           <blockquote>
            "Unilateral changes to US military laws formally granted the Pentagon extraordinary powers to intervene in a domestic "emergency" or "civil disturbance"
           </blockquote>
          </div>

               <h5>
                <a class="de">
                </a>
                <span class="cu">
                 Historically, that's a really interesting question
                </span>
                <span class="crd ntb">
                 (
                 <a href="/comments/1320245/54197010#c239?mode=alone;showrate=1#c239">
                  0+ / 0-
                 </a>
                 )
                </span>
               </h5>
               <div class="ct">
                <p>
                 It leads me to ponder what France would look like today if the revolution of 1789 hadn't included the violence that led to the dissolution of the ruling hierarchy.
                </p>
                <p>
                 Could there have been a peaceful transition into democracy with a strong emphasis on socialism?
                </p>
                <p>
                 I would think doubtful. They would have suffered pretty much the same fate we face having left in place the wealthy hierarchy after the formation of the New Deal. Such families that form a wealthy elite eventually always work their way back into oligarchical, monarchical positions of power.
                </p>
               </div>
               <div class="cf">
                <p class="sig">
                </p>
                <hr color="#CCCCCC" width="100%"/>
                <p class="sig">
                 "We must make our choice. We may have democracy, or we may have wealth concentrated in the hands of a few, but we can't have both." - Louis Brandeis

              <li id="c245">
               <div class="cx" id="cx54206145">
                <h5>
                 <a class="de">
                 </a>
                 <span class="cu">
                  It is about more than protecting corporations.
                 </span>
                 <span class="crd ntb">
                  (
                  <a href="/comments/1320245/54206145#c245?mode=alone;showrate=1#c245">
                   0+ / 0-
                  </a>
                  )
                 </span>
                </h5>
                <div class="ct">
                 <p>
                  Of course, corporations are part of the equation, but they're expecting the shit to hit the fan, with food and water shortages and anarchy in the streets. Ironically, the environmentalists will be a minor problem compared to the tea patriots who will refuse to pay taxes, hoard whatever they can get their hands on, refuse to allow relocation to their communities of people displaced by flooding or a new dust bowl (especially if they are brown), etc.
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  The environmentalists know that we are all in this together, whereas the tea patriots are all for furthering their own interests at any costs, something that they share with the corporations. To further your analogy to the French Revolution, the attitude of tea patriots and corporations will be "let them eat soylent green."
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  Of course, the government is interested in protecting its institutions and preventing chaos and subsequent dictatorship. In its attempt to preserve "freedom" and democracy, it is, ironically, eroding individual freedoms to a degree unseen in the past 80 years.
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  The environmentalists have it right - we must do what we can now to slow climate change and preserve existing water supplies. We should also be looking at the most efficient means of producing food on a massive scale - perhaps growing more drought-tolerant pants like moringa. We should look to arid areas of the third world for crops that could be cultivated in new arid regions. A good place to start this environmental initiative would be a large tax on meat, with proceeds going to environmental and agricultural research.
                 </p>

              <div class="ct">
               <p>
                I'll first admit that I'm old and worn out, at 65 going on 80, but I've been saying this for a few years now. The 1% is going to drive the other 99% into the streets. Revolution will end up being our only solution. As much as I hate the fanatical tea party types, those survivalists and prepper types, do have the right idea. We should all be ready for when the drums start beating. When the OWS protestors were so quickly and efficiently dispersed, nationwide, I knew for sure that it's coming. The results of the 2010 midterm's showed just how lazy and unconcerned the majority of sane thinkers in this country are. If there is to be any real hope of changing things around peacefully, then all sane, rational people in this country will have to turn out to vote like they never have before, this November in the midterms. We need to throw out all the right wing political hacks.

              </h5>

The killings would be approved as being critical for the maintenance of national security.

                </a>
                <span class="cu">
                 People have to have a wage
                </span>
                <span class="crd ntb">

                 <a href="/comments/1320245/54197162#c241?mode=alone;showrate=1#c241">
                  0+ / 0-

               </h5>
               <div class="ct">
                <p>
                 to be capable of buying things. There will not, and have not, always been customers. This diary really nails it. People seem to think climate change is an issue that should only concern those who love beautiful state parks and cute fuzzy animals. In reality, climate change is about the very ability of nations to sustain themselves and avoid falling into chaos similar to that in Somalia. It's about droughts, water scarcity, fields flooded, and massive collapse of economies. It is THE issue of our time, yet we-- even in the democratic party --treat it as if it's just some fringe issue.

            <li id="c240">
             <div class="cx" id="cx54197096">
              <h5>
               <a class="de">
               </a>
               <span class="cu">
                There are consumers; there are producers;
               </span>
               <span class="crd ntb">
                (
                <a href="/comments/1320245/54197096#c240?mode=alone;showrate=1#c240">
                 0+ / 0-
                </a>
                )
               </span>
              </h5>
              <div class="ct">
               <p>
                And in between there is a distribution chain. Which is not manned by slave labor, despite the determined efforts of the trucking companies, government regulators, and shipping conglomerates. Therein lie all kinds of possibilities for messing with the system, even as supposedly 'airtight' and 'theftproof' they've been trying to make their shipping systems. Or at least the salesmen of those systems have been trying to convince them they can be.
               </p>
               <p>
                Because while they have been locking down the trailer doors, the tractor has been ignored. Now, when thousands of skilled drivers are retiring or being forced out of the business because of new BMI REGULATIONS(Yes that's body mass index!!!), as well as hours of service rules that require fewer hours allowed, thus fewer miles driven per day when many over the road company drivers GET PAID BY THE MILE! HELLO! And in amounts calculated in PENNIES!
               </p>
               <p>
                Who needs to be gone from home for weeks at a time, showering in truckstops every couple of days if you're lucky? Who needs to work, sleep, and eat in or around a ten-square-foot space 24/7, being either lied to, bullied, ignored, or cheated by dispatchers, for less money than before? Who needs the mercilessly long stretches of monotony, interspersed with moments of raw terror, when a car tries to commit suicide against the front end of your vehicle? Or because of lack of funds, you get to play "Ice Road Truckers" in Iowa, or Wisconsin, or Arkansas, or Oklahoma? For the love of the work? Please.
               </p>
               <p>
                My point, and I do have one, is that here is an example of what can happen when regulation goes too far; and I am a firm believer in strong government regulation, but when it's useful and in service to the American people, not when it is used as a punching glove against a specific group that can't fight back or has no say in how the regulations are applied. There is already a nationwide shortage of truck drivers due to the corrosive effect of low pay, difficult work, some of it unpaid (ever hear of 'lumping' freight? Try doing it for free), loneliness, lack of cohesiveness, long, long hours, and the staggering monotony of just doing the job.
               </p>
               <p>
                Throw in the changes in regulation, which mean changes (drop) in pay, and the new rules that may make it impossible for overweight but otherwise healthy drivers to pass their mandated DOT physicals, and you have a recipe for what we are seeing now; a driver shortage.
               </p>
               <p>
                If people could be persuaded to understand the power of joining together, of unionizing, they could see firsthand the strength they actually have, but only in numbers, and only when buckled together, like a chain.
               </p>
               <p>
                All Corporate America can do as a workaround is to get self-driving trucks to deliver their freight, and I can see nothing but disaster associated with this. America is too big, too sprawling, and too divided to do the work that would need to be done for this to happen.
               </p>
               <p>
                People think there are 'food deserts' now? New Jersey would end up as one big warehouse like a huge combination Walmart and Costco, and that's where people from the entire Northeast would have to go to shop. Glorious. Because Corporate America would have to keep answering to its stakeholders (as well as its stateholders).
                <br/>
               </p>
              </div>

Birth control is out, one child family law doesnt work, advances in medicine to keep people alive longer are absolutely counter-productive. Finally they realize they will have to find ways to poison the 4 or 5 billion unwanted bodies so that they either die young or at least before they propagate, and those who get past that are infertile or their children die young and they die a lot sooner than they do now.

All these business-friendly products and systems will have the benefit of killing people and thus lowering the population fairly quickly. Theres not much time left, and maybe its even a bit late and they wish theyd done this sooner. I think it would take at least 40 years, and by that time even the ber-wealthy may not find any place on earth to live well.
               </p>

             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               Although it may be 90-150 years (depending upon the theoretical modeling) for some ultimate CC affects - think Greenland melting, no Arctic ice in summer, Anarctic shelf collapse, etc - we'll see potentially serious upheavals far sooner, I fear.
              </p>
              <p>
               As example, the diary mentions the US underground water levels dropping, with possible severe impact on basic crops (wheat, corn, soybeans), possibly in the next ten years. To the US, and to a lesser extent the EU, this mostly means higher food prices. To the third world, it may mean shortages, maybe even severe with riots and starvation.
              </p>
              <p>
               Add to this that the large multinational financial institutions such as Goldman Sachs, even Deutsche Bank, will exacerbate the situation and try to make profits from the shortages caused by short term CC - crop failure, water shortages, etc. - and will increase the risk of another global financial bubble ala 2008. Whether it is good or bad in terms of large governments coming to terms with CC (the US being the largest CC denier, sadly), I think even short term CC will have large problems within the next 10-20 years.
              </p>
             </div>

              <p class="sig">

                costs in the billions or trillions of dollars already (intractable droughts, eg.) But so far there is enough redundancy in the international food supply chain and the chain of causation is obscure enough that public awareness hasn't caught up with reality.

                <br/>

              <div class="ct">
               <p>
                world will face a water crisis sometime between 2030 and 2040. We can do things to change that, using wind and solar PV for electricity, eating less beef/cheese, etc.

             <li id="c238">
              <div class="cx" id="cx54196696">
               <h5>
                <a class="de">
                </a>
                <span class="cu">
                 None of that makes any sense.
                </span>
                <span class="crd ntb">
                 (
                 <a href="/comments/1320245/54196696#c238?mode=alone;showrate=1#c238">
                  0+ / 0-
                 </a>
                 )
                </span>
               </h5>
               <div class="ct">
                <p>
                 "The world" isn't one place. Water availability in different parts of the world differ by many orders of magnitude. It makes no sense to say "the world" is going to suffer a water shortage at a specific time. Heck, in places a reasonable distance from the ocean (a large chunk if not the majority of the world's population), cooling water availability is for all effective purposes unlimited.
                </p>
                <p>
                 The claim that "it doesn't matter what price" doesn't make any sense either. If you're willing to pay more for power, not only can you afford to ship in more remote water supplies for cooling, but you can altogether switch to air-cooling. Everything has a price. And the prices involved here, while relevant, are not exorbitant.
                </p>
                <p>
                 Of course, this leaves out the obvious solution, which is to do as we do in Iceland and
                 <i>
                  stop treating hot water as a waste product and start treating it as a value-added product
                 </i>
                 . Distribute it to homes and businesses, customers will
                 <i>
                  pay you
                 </i>
                 for the privilege of not having having to have a water heater or furnace and not have to pay exorbitant rates for home and water heating. You save profit, they profit, and the environment profits.
                 <br/>
                </p>
               </div>
               <div class="cf">
                <p class="sig">
                 The day I'll consider justice blind is the day that a rape defendant's claim of "She consented to the sex" is treated by the same legal standards as a robbery defendant's claim of "He consented to give me the money": as an affirmative defense.
                </p>

                <h5>
                 <a class="de">
                 </a>
                 <span class="cu">
                  We can, and hopefully will, innovate.  The problem
                 </span>
                 <span class="crd ntb">
                  (
                  <a href="/comments/1320245/54226605#c250?mode=alone;showrate=1#c250">
                   0+ / 0-
                  </a>
                  )
                 </span>
                </h5>
                <div class="ct">
                 <p>
                  with your arguments is that infrastructure, new tech, etc. takes planning (time) and funding (money), which aren't always available. My intent was to convey the fact that we need to start planning and implementing solutions for areas that we suspect or already know will be problematic.
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  The point is that very little water on the planet is potable, and that resource will become more scarce as water demand, energy demand, food demand increases. So, we should be thinking about water security (conservation, preventing pollution, reuse, etc.)
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  I like your idea about using the hot water as a resource. Where I live (Chicago), most of our electricity is either from coal or nuclear thermoelectric plants. We pay a lot to use gas to heat our water and homes. We could be doing better. A few people use solar thermal to heat their homes and water (which reduces their gas bill on average 85%).
                 </p>

               <div class="ct">
                <p>
                 I agree: our government is of, by, and for corporate interests. Such corporations own most food production in the USA, and are beginning to own water resources as well (formally owned by the "we the people" government, now being privatized for "greater efficiency").
                </p>
                <p>
                 Global climate change will effect food production and water resources, and should any scarcities occur, the DoD will need to step in and protect those corporate interests and quell whatever public disturbances arise due to lack of food and water, or to tamp down political unrest.
                </p>
                <p>
                 In my opinion, those rural fascists are less likely to be effected by food and water scarcity: they are closer to the land and therefore closer to food and water resources. I think the greater threat to public order will be in the cities, where food and water limits will be felt more urgently.
                </p>
                <p>
                 Conservative rural groups may stage small disturbances for political reasons, but civil unrest over food and water limitations will be much worse in the cities.
                </p>
               </div>

                <div class="ct">
                 <p>
                  i think what OO is saying is that the rural areas will be the larger problem because it will take much more time and effort to "rein them in."
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  yes, the urban areas will explode early--food and water shortages will be felt there first, and hard. but, as OO noted, they can be contained. actually, they are somewhat "self contained," meaning, even if they want to leave, they don't have the means for mass evacuation, and...where would they go? to the rural areas?
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  well, then the rural areas will HELP the government/corporation to end the problem. the righties in the rural areas will turn their guns on those fleeing the cities. if some in the city DO make it into rural areas, in general, they are ill-equipped to survive. those old stereotypes--the "country bumpkin" can't survive well in a dense, urban area that has clear social divisions which the bumpkin both doesn't know about and doesn't understand. the "city slicker" can't survive well "out on the farm," because the slicker doesn't understand farming (or, in most cases, even gardening) or hunting (for food) or "living off the land."
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  so, yes, the stuff hits the fan first in the urban areas. the police make some attempts, initially, to calm the civil unrest; but then the military moves in. probably to set up snipers to take out some of the "worst problems," but more to set up and enforce a blockade. they'll try to confine the problem/s and not let people leave/escape. essentially, they'll lock the residents into their city and/or neighborhood, and let them fight amongst themselves for what little food and water is allowed in.
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  then comes the REAL problem, as OO said--trying to contain/control/manage the rural areas.
                 </p>

         <li id="c268">
          <div class="cx" id="cx56901339">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             Our best national defense against climate change
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1320245/56901339#c268?mode=alone;showrate=1#c268">
              0+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             has to be mitigation which has to be about 90% replacing fossil fuel with renewable energy and 10% capture of storage of CO2 already in ambient air. We will also needs lots of adaptation to climate change--mostly desalination of seawater for irrigation, and using the desalinated seawater as efficiently as possible. Israel uses mostly desalinated seawater--they also grow most of what they grow in greenhouses to cut down on loss of water to evaporation. I am sure it is technically feasible to grow food in 10 feet underground greenhouses with desalinated seawater and also recycled water from sewage after it has been desalinated for use as domestic water supply and using led grow lights powered by solar power. I am also sure it is NOT economically feasible to come anywhere near feeding our world population that way.
            </p>
           </div>

        <li id="c8">
         <div class="cx" id="cx54185636">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Well, there won't be anti-government
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1320245/54185636#c8?mode=alone;showrate=1#c8">
             13+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:83269">
              gerald 1969
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:175799">
              Pakalolo
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:212680">

             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:50679">
              Superpole
             </a>

             <a href="/user/uid:59515">

             </a>

             </a>
             ,

             </a>
             ,

             </a>

             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:1028289">
              1500honda
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            activism when people perceive that the government is us, we the people. It's only authoritarians and petty tyrants that have to worry about the people turning on them when they tighten the screws too much.
           </p>
           <p>
            The lesson of the '60s seems to have been totally misunderstood. People don't insist on their civil rights when they are on the edge of starvation. People seek greater control of their lives when their material condition is such that they have energy and time to spare for higher objectives.
           </p>
           <p>
            The civil and equal rights agenda of the '60s wasn't in response to the great depression, but to the promise held out by the victory over oppression in Europe and the Far East for the spread of democracy and human rights. The mistaken notion that material deprivation was the cause of social unrest has prompted an agenda of material bribery on the cheap and now the banksters are distressed that the millenials aren't "buying" it. That people put more store on being respected than on having a full belly simply doesn't register with people who respond to their "gut."
           </p>
           <p>
            Do we have a lot of instinct-driven people in our military? You bet. That's who's attracted to a top-down organization where personal responsibility simply means that persons are willing and able to respond to external directives.
           </p>

                </p>
                <p>
                 And, although I see you acknowledge this in another comment, so this isn't news, I think it is important to note here that there is an obvious military/strategic objective when it comes to energy efficiency -- any student of WWII knows that the need to get fuel to the front lines, for example, created a great deal of vulnerabilities and headaches (to put it mildly). Using less fuel or alternative fuel that is more readily available accomplishes a big strategic objective.
                </p>
                <p>
                 Whether or not I cheer for the military, they're going to do this anyway, but I don't want to give too much credit when there is a military as well as a PR imperative for what they are doing here. Even if it has positive effects on their energy consumption, so, yay for that, we're really just talking about an organization that mobilizes massive amounts of energy to accomplish goals that are, the vast majority of the time, goals that I do not support. It doesn't actually take a lot of fuel to defend the US homeland. Not compared to maintaining a massive American military presence around the world that, as others have pointed out, has a lot to do with safeguarding supplies of dirty energy.

            ... the so-called fan. Already some small clusters of islands have been permanently evacuated, with more to follow. Whole island nations will
            <em>
             cease to exist
            </em>
            as the oceans continue to rise. By the end of this century, refugees from rising sea levels are likely to reach to 100s of millions. That's hard times for Bangladesh, where India has already been building a border wall to keep them out. But it's rough times everywhere else, too, because a big chunk of the world's people live on the coasts. Although it's rarely mentioned, drought has driven the crisis and carnage in Syria. Climate change issues contribute significantly to many issues in the world already.

            The Congressional Research Service (CRS) has issued a report that informed Congress that it has created 439 new criminal offenses between 2008 and 2013. This staggering figure gives a glimpse into
            <strong>
             the rapid criminalization of America
            </strong>
            where there are, by some estimates, around 4,500 federal offenses alone and tens of thousands of more on the state and federal levels. Politicians continue to add crimes, which tend to be popular with voters and do not require immediate budget demands (though they add huge costs not just in enforcement but the costs of citizens themselves in being pulled into the criminal justice system).
           </blockquote>

            <br/>
            I believe US Navy is working on sustainable substitutes for petroleum products. This article sounds like the need is urgent. Most ways to make liquid fuel sustainably involve capturing CO2, separating water into hydrogen and oxygen, separating CO2 into carbon and oxygen, and combining the hydrogen and carbon to make hydrocarbon fuel. This recycles the carbon in CO2. Once US Navy succeeds in developing a sustainable replacement for petroleum close enough to cost-competitive with petroleum to interest our too big to fail oil firms, it will be at least some help in mitigating global warming.

            </span>

             system leads to wilder fluctuation- that's true of any self-regulating system. As the fluctuations we're likely to see are weather events, that explains
            </p>
            <blockquote>
             tsunamis, typhoons, hurricanes, tornadoes,
            </blockquote>
            Warmer air temperatures mean higher absolute humidity- there's just plain more water in the air. This means heavier rainfall in some places, which, by changing water distribution, can cause
            <blockquote>
             earthquakes and other natural catastrophes...
            </blockquote>

              I'll accept...
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">

            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              ...localized earthquakes might be caused by stupendous amounts of water entering previously deep-fractured ground (Oklahoma leaps to mind) but earthquakes of the magnitude needed to cause a tsunami are miles deep and usually generated at plate boundaries.              <p>
              The exception would be
              <a href="http://geology.com/records/biggest-tsunami.shtml">
               small landslide tsunamis.
              </a>
              <a href="http://blogs.agu.org/landslideblog/2013/12/13/canary-islands-tsunami/">

The US Army believes in detailed planning down to the individual soldier. Im not kidding. Every soldier is supposed to have a GOTWA - GOTWA stands for (G) where I'm Going, (O) Others I'm taking, (T) Time of my return, (W) What to do if I don't return, (A) Actions to take if I'm hit or Actions to take if you're hit (5 point contingency plan). I could walk up to any soldier in Afghanistan right now and ask them for their GOTWA and 9 out of 10 would have it ready. 8 out of 10 could tell me their squad leaders GOTWA and 6 out of 10 could tell me the Platoon Sergeants.

  <script type="text/javascript">
   if($.cookie('meter_user') !== 'false') {
		if ($('#ePostButton').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('#ePostButton').click(function() { 
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Posted Comment', { 
		      repliedTo: $(this).parents('#eP').prevAll('.cb').children('a').first().html() 
		    });
		  });
		}
		
		if (!!document.location.pathname.match('activate') &amp;&amp; $('h1:contains("Welcome to Daily Kos!")').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Signed Up');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname === '/') {
		  if (document.location.hostname.match(/^www|^dailykos/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Front Page');
		  } else {
		    var subdomain = document.location.hostname.split('.')[0];
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Vertical', { name: subdomain });
		  }
		}
		
		if ($('.ratinate').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.ratinate').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Comment') });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-addRating.type-story').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-addRating.type-story').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Story') });
		}
		
		if ($("form[action$='publishHow']").length &gt; 0) {
		  $(document).ready(function() {
			  $('.type-story.do-publishHow').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Published Story') });
			 });
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/user/) &amp;&amp; $('li.here').length &gt; 0) {
		  if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Stream/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Stream', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Profile/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Profile', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:last li.here').children('a').html().match(/My Recent Replies/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Replies');
		  }
		} 
		
		if ($('#search-tabbed').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Searched');
		}
		
		if ($('#group-tables').length &gt; 0) {
			DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Groups List');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/groups/) &amp;&amp; $("#group_info").length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Group', { name: $('.name').children('a').text() });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').click(function() {
		    var nickName;
		    nickName = ($(this).prev('a').html() || $('.name a').html())
		    if ($(this).hasClass('do-hotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Followed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    } else if ($(this).hasClass('do-unHotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Unfollowed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    }    
		  });
		}
	}
  </script>
